7.093z"></path></svg></a></div><div class="sidebar_titlesWrapper__QElZv py-16" id="sidebarContent"><p class="sidebar_navTopLabel__xl_5k">Contents</p><ol><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK sidebar_linkActive__wuL2e" href="#introduction" rel="noopener">Introduction</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#key-takeaways" rel="noopener">Key Takeaways</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#campaign" rel="noopener">Campaign</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#infection-chain-analysis" rel="noopener">Infection Chain Analysis</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#tracking-the-threat-actor" rel="noopener">Tracking The Threat Actor</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#conclusion" rel="noopener">Conclusion</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#zscaler-sandbox-coverage" rel="noopener">Zscaler Sandbox Coverage</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#indicators-of-compromise--iocs-" rel="noopener">Indicators Of Compromise (IOCs)</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#more-blogs" rel="noopener">More blogs</a></li></ol></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0  hidden lg:flex" style="border-bottom:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0 flex lg:hidden" style="border-bottom:none;border-top:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden lg:block" style="margin-bottom:-0px"></div><div class="" id="introduction"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Introduction</h2><p>Zscaler ThreatLabz research team observed a new campaign targeting a Government organization in which the threat actors utilized a new Command &amp; Control (C2) framework named <a href="https://github.com/HavocFramework/Havoc"><strong><u>Havoc</u></strong></a>. While C2 frameworks are prolific, the open-source Havoc framework is an advanced post-exploitation command and control framework capable of bypassing the most current and updated version of Windows 11 defender due to the implementation of advanced evasion techniques such as indirect syscalls and sleep obfuscation.</p><p>The technical analysis that follows provides an overview of recently discovered attack campaign targeting government organization using Havoc and reveals how it can be leveraged by the threat actors in various campaigns.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="key-takeaways"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Key Takeaways</h2><ul><li>Observed New threat campaign leveraging the open-source Havoc C2 framework targeting Government organization</li><li>Analysis of Havoc Demon - Implant generated via the Havoc framework<ul><li>ShellCode Loader:<ul><li>Disables the Event Tracing for Windows (ETW) to evade detection mechanisms.</li><li>Decrypts and executes the shellcode via CreateThreadpoolWait()</li></ul></li><li>KaynLdr Shellcode:<ul><li>Reflectively loads the Havoc’s Demon DLL without the DOS and NT headers to evade detection.</li><li>Performs API hashing routine to resolve virtual addresses of various NTAPI&#x27;s by using modified DJB2 hashing algorithm</li></ul></li></ul></li><li>Demon DLL:<ul><li>Parsing configuration files</li><li>Usage of Sleep Obfuscation Techniques</li><li>Communication with the CnC Server - CheckIn Request and Command Execution</li><li>Performs In-Direct Syscalls and Return Address Stack Spoofing and more</li></ul></li><li>Performed tracking of the threat actor based on infrastructure analysis and opsec blunders where we gathered and analyzed the screenshots of the threat actors machine from the CnC due to self-compromise.</li></ul>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="campaign"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Campaign</h2><p>In the beginning of January, this year, we discovered an executable named “pics.exe” in the Zscaler Cloud targeting a Government Organization. The executable was downloaded from a remote server: “146[.]190[.]48[.]229” as shown in the screenshot below</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_1.png" alt="Fig 1. Campaign - Zscaler Cloud" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 1. Campaign - Zscaler Cloud</em></p><p>Let us now examine the infection chain used by the threat actors in the following campaign to deliver the Havoc Demon on the target machine.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="infection-chain-analysis"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Infection Chain Analysis</h2><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_2.jpeg" alt="Fig 2. Infection chain" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 2. Infection chain</em></p><p>The infection chain utilized by the threat actors for delivering the <strong>Havoc Demon</strong> on the target machines commences with a ZIP Archive named “ZeroTwo.zip” consisting of two files “character.scr” and “Untitled Document.docx” as shown in the screenshot below.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_3.png" alt="Fig 3. ZIP Archive" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 3. ZIP Archive</em></p><p> </p><p>Here the “Untitled Document.docx” is a document consisting of paragraphs regarding the “ZeroTwo” which is a fictional character in the Japanese anime television series Darling in the Franxx.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_4.png" alt="Fig 4. Contents of the Document bundled in the ZIP Archive" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 4. Contents of the Document bundled in the ZIP Archive</em></p><p> </p><p>Further the screen saver file “character.scr” is basically a downloader commissioned to download and execute the Havoc Demon Agent on the victim machine. The Downloader binary is compiled using a BAT to EXE converter “BAT2EXE” which allows users to convert Batch scripts into executables as shown in the screenshot below. The BAT2EXE argument can be seen in the downloader binary.</p><p> </p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_5.png" alt="Fig 5. BAT2EXE argument used in the downloader binary" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 5. BAT2EXE argument used in the downloader binary</em></p><p><br/>Once executed the BAT2EXE compiled binary loads and decrypts the Batch Script from the .rsrc section as shown in the screenshot below.</p><p> </p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_6.png" alt="Fig 6. Decrypted BAT Script" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 6. Decrypted BAT Script</em></p><p>The binary then writes and executes the decrypted BAT script from the Temp folder as shown in the image below.</p><p> </p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_7.png" alt="Fig 7. Decrypted BAT Script written in the Temp folder" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 7. Decrypted BAT Script written in the Temp folder</em></p><p> </p><p>The Decrypted BAT Script upon execution performs the following tasks:</p><p>Checks whether “teste.exe” exists in the Temp folder, if not, it downloads the final payload from http[:]//146[.]190[.]48[.]229/pics.exe and saves it as “seethe.exe” in the Temp folder via Invoke-WebRequest and then executes it using “start seethe.exe”</p><p> </p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_8.png" alt="Fig 8. Downloads the final payload “pics.exe” from remote server via Invoke-WebRequest" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 8. Downloads the final payload “pics.exe” from remote server via Invoke-WebRequest</em></p><p> </p><p>Then it checks whether “testv.exe” exists in the Temp folder, if not, it downloads an image from “https[:]//i[.]pinimg[.]com/originals/d4/20/66/d42066e9f8c4b75a0723b8778c370f1d.jpg” and saves it as images.jpg in the Temp folder and opens it using images.jpg.</p><p> </p><p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_9.png" alt="Image" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/>      </p><p><em>Fig 9. Downloads a JPG image from pinimg[.]com</em></p><p>The following image of the “Zero Two” character was downloaded from pinimg[.]com &amp; executed in order to conceal the actual execution and malicious activities performed by the final payload.</p><p> </p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_10.png" alt="Fig 10. Zero Two Image downloaded from pinimg[.]com" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 10. Zero Two Image downloaded from pinimg[.]com</em></p><p> </p><p>Before analyzing the final payload, let’s take a look at another similar Downloader compiled via BAT2EXE named “ihatemylife.exe”, in this case, the decrypted Batch script downloads the final payload from “https[:]//ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga/image[.]exe” using Invoke-WebRequest alongside the payload it also downloads an image to conceal the malicious activities as shown in the screenshot below. </p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_11.png" alt="Fig 11. Decrypted Batch scripts downloads the final payload from https[:]//ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 11. Decrypted Batch scripts downloads the final payload from https[:]//ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga</em></p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_12.png" alt="Fig 12. Image Downloaded by the Batch Script to conceal malicious activities" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 12. Image Downloaded by the Batch Script to conceal malicious activities</em></p><p>Now let’s analyze the final In-the-Wild “Havoc Demon” payload which was downloaded via the Downloader named “character.scr” from http[:]//146[.]190[.]48[.]229/pics.exe as explained previously.</p><p><strong>Havoc Demon</strong> is the implant generated via the <a href="https://github.com/HavocFramework/Havoc"><strong><u>Havoc Framework</u></strong></a> - which is a modern and malleable post-exploitation command and control framework created by @C5pider.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_13.png" alt="Fig 13. The Havoc Framework" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 13. The Havoc Framework</em></p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_14.png" alt="Fig 14. Havoc Framework - Interface" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 14. Havoc Framework - Interface</em></p><h3><br/>Shellcode Loader</h3><p>The Downloaded payload “pics.exe” is the <strong>“Shellcode Loader”</strong> which is signed using Microsoft’s Digital certificate as shown in the screenshot below.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_15.png" alt="Fig 15. Microsoft Signed Executable" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 15. Microsoft Signed Executable</em></p><p> </p><p>Upon execution the Shellcode Loader at first disables the Event Tracing for Windows (ETW) by patching the WinApi “EtwEventWrite()” which is responsible for writing an event. ETW Patching process:</p><ul><li>Retrieves module handle of ntdll.dll via GetModuleHandleA</li><li>Retrieves address of EtwEventWrite via GetProcAddress</li></ul><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_16.png" alt="Fig 16. Fetches the address of EtwEventWrite" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 16. Fetches the address of EtwEventWrite</em></p><ul><li>Further it changes the protection of the region via VirtualProtect and then overwrites the first 4 bytes of the EtwEventWrite with following bytes: 0x48,0x33,0xc0,0xc3 (xor rax,rax | ret)</li></ul><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/fig_17.png" alt="Fig 17. Overwriting bytes to patch EtwEventWrite" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 17. Overwriting bytes to patch EtwEventWrite</em></p><p>By patching the EtwEventWrite function the ETW will not be able to write any events thus disabling the ETW.</p><p>Then the payload AES decrypts the shellcode using CryptDecrypt() as shown in the screenshot below - in this case the Algorithm ID used is “0x00006610” - AES256</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_18.png" alt="Fig 18. AES Decrypts the Shellcode via CryptDecrypt" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 18. AES Decrypts the Shellcode via CryptDecrypt</em></p><p>Once the Shellcode is decrypted, the Shellcode is executed via <strong>CreateThreadpoolWait()</strong> where at first it creates an event object in a signaled state via CreateEventA(), then allocates RWX memory via VirtualAlloc() and writes the Shellcode in the allocated memory. Further it creates a wait object using CreateThreadpoolWait, here the first argument - callback function is set to the address of the shellcode. Then it set’s the wait object via the NtApi “TpSetWait” and at last calls the WaitForSingleObject which once executed checks if the waitable object is in signaled state, as our event was created in signaled state the callback function is been executed i.e the decrypted shellcode is been executed and the control flow is been transferred to the shellcode.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_19.png" alt="Fig 19. Shellcode execution via CreateThreadpoolWait" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 19. Shellcode execution via CreateThreadpoolWait</em></p><p> </p><h3>KaynLdr - Shellcode</h3><p>The Shellcode in this case is the “KaynLdr” which is commissioned to reflectively load the Havoc’s Demon DLL implant by calling its entrypoint function. Once the Shellcode is executed it retrieves the image base of the Demon DLL which is embedded in the shellcode itself by executing the following inline assembly function called KaynCaller.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_20.png" alt="Fig 20. Retrieves the Image Base of the Embedded Demon DLL" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 20. Retrieves the Image Base of the Embedded Demon DLL</em></p><p>Further the KaynLdr performs the API Hashing routine in order to resolve the virtual addresses of various NTAPI’s by walking the export address table of the ntdll.dll (Function: LdrFunctionAddr) and initially the virtual address of the NTDLL.dll is been retrieved by walking the Process Environment Block (Function: LdrFunctionAddr) as shown in the screenshot below</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_21.png" alt="Fig 21. API Hashing Routine used by Havoc Demon" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 21. API Hashing Routine used by Havoc Demon</em></p><p>Here the hashing algorithm used is a modified version of “DJB2” algorithm based on the constant “5381” or “0x1505” as shown in the screenshot below.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_22.png" alt="Fig 22. Modified DJB2 Hashing Algorithm used in the API Hashing Routine" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 22. Modified DJB2 Hashing Algorithm used in the API Hashing Routine</em></p><p>Virtual Addresses for the following module and NTAPI’s are retrieved by using the API Hashing routine where the hardcoded DJB2 hashes are compared with the dynamically generated hash.</p><p> </p><div><table><tbody><tr><td>0x70e61753</td><td>ntdll.dll</td></tr><tr><td>0x9e456a43</td><td>LdrLoadDll</td></tr><tr><td>0xf783b8ec</td><td>NtAllocateVirtualMemory</td></tr><tr><td>0x50e92888</td><td>NtProtectVirtualMemory</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p> </p><p>Further the Embedded Demon DLL is memory mapped and the base relocations are calculated if required in an allocated memory page procured by calling the NtAllocateVirtualMemory(). Also the page protections are changed via multiple calls to NtProtectVirtualMemory as shown below.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_23.png" alt="Fig 23. Memory Mapping of the embedded Demon DLL" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 23. Memory Mapping of the embedded Demon DLL</em></p><p>The Demon DLL is memory mapped in the Allocated memory without the DOS and NT Headers in order to evade detection mechanisms.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_24.png" alt="Fig 24. Demon DLL is memory mapped without DOS and NT Headers" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 24. Demon DLL is memory mapped without DOS and NT Headers</em></p><p>Now once the Demon DLL is memory mapped the KaynDllMain i.e the entrypoint of the DLL is executed by the KaynLdr as shown below, from there on the control is transferred to the Havoc Demon DLL Implant.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_25.png" alt="Fig 25. Entrypoint of the Demon DLL is been executed by the KaynLdr" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 25. Entrypoint of the Demon DLL is been executed by the KaynLdr</em></p><p> </p><h3>Analysis of Havoc Demon DLL</h3><p>The entrypoint of the Havoc Demon DLL is executed by the KaynLdr as discussed previously. Now as the Havoc Demon has many features, we will only focus on a few of them in the following blog, as the features can be deduced from its source at: <a href="https://github.com/HavocFramework/Havoc"><u>https://github.com/HavocFramework/Havoc</u></a></p><p>So once the Havoc Demon is been executed there are four functions which are been executed by the DemonMain(): </p><ul><li><strong>DemonInit</strong></li><li><strong>DemonMetaData</strong></li><li><strong>DemonConfig</strong></li><li><strong>DemonRoutine</strong></li></ul><p>The DemonInit is the initialization function which</p><ul><li>Retrieves the virtual addresses of functions from modules such as ntdll.dll/kernel32.dll by calling the API Hashing Routine discussed previously.</li><li>Retrevies Syscall stubs for various NTAPI’s</li><li>Loads various Modules via walking the PEB with stacked strings</li><li>Initialize Session and Config Objects such as Demon AgentID, ProcessArch etc.</li></ul><p>Now let’s understand how the Configuration is being parsed via the DemonConfig() function.</p><p>The Demon’s Configuration is been stored in the .data section as shown in the screenshot below</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_26.png" alt="Fig 26. Demon Configuration stored in the .data section" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 26. Demon Configuration stored in the .data section</em></p><p>The DemonConfig function parses the configuration by indexing the various required values from the config. Following is the configuration for the Demon DLL used in the campaign.</p><p><strong>Configuration:</strong></p><ul><li>Sleep: 2 (0x2)</li><li>Injection: <ul><li>Allocate: Native/Syscall (0x2)</li><li>Execute: Native/Syscall (0x2)</li></ul></li><li>Spawn:<ul><li>x64: C:\Windows\System32\notepad.exe</li><li>x86: C:\Windows\SysWOW64\notepad.exe</li></ul></li><li>Sleep Obfuscation Technique: Ekko (0x2)</li><li>Method: POST</li><li>Host: 146[.]190[.]48[.]229</li><li>Transport Secure: TRUE</li><li>UserAgent: Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 6.1; WOW64) AppleWebKit/537/36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/96.0.4664.110 Safari/537.36</li></ul><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_27.png" alt="Fig 27. Demon Configuration - Host (CnC) and UserAgent parsed" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 27. Demon Configuration - Host (CnC) and UserAgent parsed</em></p><p>The <strong>DemonRoutine()</strong> function is the main loop for the malware, it is responsible for connecting to the command and control (C2) server, waiting for tasks from the server, executing those tasks, and then waiting again for more tasks and running indefinitely. It does the following things:</p><ul><li>First, it checks if it is connected to the C2 server. If not, it calls TransportInit() to connect to the server.</li><li>If the connection is successful, it enters the CommandDispatcher() function, which is responsible for a task routine which parses the tasks and executes them until there are no more tasks in the queue.</li><li>If the malware is unable to connect to the C2 server, it will keep trying to connect to the server again</li></ul><p>Now let’s understand how it connects to the TransportInit function:</p><p>TransportInit() is responsible for connecting to the C2 server and establishing a session. It first sends the AES encrypted MetaData packet i.e the Check-in request generated via the DemonMetaData() function through the PackageTransmit() function, which could be sending data over HTTP or SMB, depending on the value of the TRANSPORT_HTTP or TRANSPORT_SMB macro. If the transmission is successful, it then decrypts the received data using AES encryption with a given key and initialization vector on the TeamServer. The decrypted data is then checked against the agent&#x27;s ID, and if they match, the session is marked as connected and the function returns true.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_28.png" alt="Fig 28. Metadata Structure - CheckIn Request" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 28. Metadata Structure - CheckIn Request</em></p><p>TransportSend() is used to send data to the C2 server. It takes a pointer to the data and its size as input, and optionally returns received data and its size.It then creates a buffer with the data to be sent, and depending on the transport method, it either sends the data over HTTP or SMB.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_29.jpeg" alt="Fig 29. TransportSend Function Arguments With Encrypted Data of the Check In request" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 29. TransportSend Function Arguments With Encrypted Data of the Check In request</em></p><p>On the Teamserver end the CheckIn request with the metadata packet is been decrypted and showcased on the terminal with both encrypted and decrypted details of packets sent and received.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_30.png" alt="Fig 30. Check In Request - Metadata packet parsed by the Team Server" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 30. Check In Request - Metadata packet parsed by the Team Server</em></p><p><strong>Command Execution:</strong></p><p>After the demon is deployed successfully on the target’s machine, the server is able to execute various commands on the target system. If the command &quot;whoami&quot; is issued to the payload, it would trigger the execution of the command and display the current user running the session.The server logs the command and its response upon execution.<br/><br/><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_31.png" alt="Fig 31. Command execution using Havoc GUI" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><em>Fig 31. Command execution using Havoc GUI</em></p><p>Once the command is executed on the victim machine, the command output is AES Encrypted and then sent to the CnC server, which is then decrypted by the TeamServer as shown in the screenshot below.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_32.jpeg" alt="Fig 32. Command Output Logs parsed by the TeamServer" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 32. Command Output Logs parsed by the TeamServer</em></p><p><strong>List Of Commands:</strong></p><p>The specific commands available in Havoc will depend on the version and configuration of the framework, but some common commands that are often included in C2 frameworks include:<br/><br/><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_33.png" alt="Fig 33. Commands List" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><em>Fig 33. Commands List</em></p><p>Further the Demon implements various techniques mentioned below which can be analyzed from the <a href="https://github.com/HavocFramework/Havoc"><u>source</u></a>:</p><ul><li>Return Address Stack Spoofing</li><li>In-Direct Syscalls</li><li>Sleep Masking Techniques<ul><li>Ekko</li><li>FOLIAGE</li><li>WaitForSingleObjectEx</li></ul></li></ul>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="tracking-the-threat-actor"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Tracking The Threat Actor - Infrastructure and Opsec Blunders</h2><p>The domain name “ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga” from where the final havoc demon payload “image.exe” is downloaded in this case resolves to the IP Address “146[.]190[.]48[.]229” - which is the IP address from where the final payload “pics.exe” was downloaded via the URL: http[:]//146[.]190[.]48[.]229/pics.exe previously. Whilst performing the infrastructure analysis we came across an open-directory on the server “ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga” where multiple demon &amp; metasploit payloads along with internal logs and screenshots were hosted as shown in the screenshot below.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_34.jpeg" alt="Fig 34. Open Directory - “ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga”" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 34. Open Directory - “ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga”</em></p><p>While examining the files on the open directory, we stumbled upon a HTML file named “NFcmoOSI.html”. The file displayed a screenshot of the threat actor’s machine as illustrated below.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_35.jpeg" alt="Fig 35. Tracking the threat actor - Metasploit Screenshare" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 35. Tracking the threat actor - Metasploit Screenshare</em></p><p>Based on our analysis, the threat actor detonated the meterpreter payload on its own machine and then used the CnC Server to initiate the Metasploit screenshare command.This action generated a file named &quot;NFcmoOSI.html&quot; on the server which contained a screenshot of the machine being shared along with the Target IP, Start Time and status of the screenshare.</p><p>Further we were able to gather following information from the threat actors machine screenshot as highlighted below where the initial payload used in our campaign was present on the TAs machine along with the Havoc Demon implant and much more.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_36.jpeg" alt="Fig 36. Tracking the threat actor - Machine Screenshot" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 36. Tracking the threat actor - Machine Screenshot</em></p><p>Now based on the Target IP (i.e the threat actors IP) the location of that IP seems to be in New York, USA. Additionally, the temperature at the time of the screenshot: 1/12/2023 7:28PM was 50° Fahrenheit (Cloudy), after mapping the historical weather data of New York at that specific time we found that the average temperature was approx close to 50° degrees Fahrenheit during that time period.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_37.png" alt="Fig 37. Tracking the threat actor - Temperature " loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 37. Tracking the threat actor - Temperature </em></p><p>Alongside, we came across a log file named “wget-log” which consists of the wget log where the Document lure “Untitled-document.docx” was downloaded from the DropBox URL: “https://www.dropbox.com/scl/fi/hnlvrwbl9v2zadl356mt3/Untitled-document.docx”</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_38.jpeg" alt="Fig 38. Tracking the threat actor - wget logs" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 38. Tracking the threat actor - wget logs</em></p><p>Also the HTML pages “index.nginx-debian.html” and “login.nginx-debian.html” are under-development Twitter phishing pages as shown in the screenshot below.</p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_39.png" alt="Fig 39. Twitter Phishing Pages hosted on “ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga”" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p><em>Fig 39. Twitter Phishing Pages hosted on “ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga”</em></p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="conclusion"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Conclusion</h2><p>The Havoc C2 framework campaign highlights the importance of proper cybersecurity measures in today&#x27;s digital world. The use of payloads and CnC servers to execute malicious commands and gather sensitive information showcases the ever-present threat of cyber attacks. The scenario described in the blog demonstrates the capabilities of such campaigns and the need for organizations to stay vigilant and protect their systems. With the rise of technology, the need for robust security solutions becomes increasingly vital, and organizations must take proactive steps to ensure the safety of their systems and data.</p>

Cloud Sandbox Report</em></p><p>Zscaler&#x27;s multilayered cloud security platform detects indicators, as shown below:</p><p><a href="https://threatlibrary.zscaler.com/threats/3e991d3d-0ad0-4188-9315-acca9e3baf4c"><strong><u>Win64.Backdoor.HavocC2</u></strong></a></p>

CheckIn Request and Command Execution\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ePerforms In-Direct Syscalls and Return Address Stack Spoofing and more\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ePerformed tracking of the threat actor based on infrastructure analysis and opsec blunders where we gathered and analyzed the screenshots of the threat actors machine from the CnC due to self-compromise.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\n"},"fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":null}]},{"__typename":"ParagraphBlogContainer","fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":"Campaign","fieldReusableParagraphs":[{"__typename":"ParagraphWysiwygModule","fieldWysiwygModContent":{"__typename":"Text","value":"\u003ch2\u003eCampaign\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the beginning of January, this year, we discovered an executable named “pics.exe” in the Zscaler Cloud targeting a Government Organization. The Downloader binary is compiled using a BAT to EXE converter “BAT2EXE” which allows users to convert Batch scripts into executables as shown in the screenshot below. The BAT2EXE argument can be seen in the downloader binary.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_5.png\" alt=\"Fig 5. Decrypted BAT Script written in the Temp folder\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 7. Decrypted BAT Script written in the Temp folder\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Decrypted BAT Script upon execution performs the following tasks:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eChecks whether “teste.exe” exists in the Temp folder, if not, it downloads the final payload from http[:]//146[.]190[.]48[.]229/pics.exe and saves it as “seethe.exe” in the Temp folder via Invoke-WebRequest and then executes it using “start seethe.exe”\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_8.png\" alt=\"Fig 8. Downloads a JPG image from pinimg[.]com\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe following image of the “Zero Two” character was downloaded from pinimg[.]com \u0026amp; executed in order to conceal the actual execution and malicious activities performed by the final payload.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_10.png\" alt=\"Fig 10. Zero Two Image downloaded from pinimg[.]com\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBefore analyzing the final payload, let’s take a look at another similar Downloader compiled via BAT2EXE named “ihatemylife.exe”, in this case, the decrypted Batch script downloads the final payload from “https[:]//ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga/image[.]exe” using Invoke-WebRequest alongside the payload it also downloads an image to conceal the malicious activities as shown in the screenshot below. \u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_11.png\" alt=\"Fig 11. Decrypted Batch scripts downloads the final payload from https[:]//ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_12.png\" alt=\"Fig 12. Image Downloaded by the Batch Script to conceal malicious activities\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 12. Image Downloaded by the Batch Script to conceal malicious activities\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNow let’s analyze the final In-the-Wild “Havoc Demon” payload which was downloaded via the Downloader named “character.scr” from http[:]//146[.]190[.]48[.]229/pics.exe as explained previously.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHavoc Demon\u003c/strong\u003e is the implant generated via the \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/HavocFramework/Havoc\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u003cu\u003eHavoc Framework\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e - which is a modern and malleable post-exploitation command and control framework created by @C5pider.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_13.png\" alt=\"Fig 13. The Havoc Framework\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 13. The Havoc Framework\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_14.png\" alt=\"Fig 14. Havoc Framework - Interface\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 14. Havoc Framework - Interface\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cbr\u003eShellcode Loader\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Downloaded payload “pics.exe” is the \u003cstrong\u003e“Shellcode Loader”\u003c/strong\u003e which is signed using Microsoft’s Digital certificate as shown in the screenshot below.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_15.png\" alt=\"Fig 15. Microsoft Signed Executable\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 15. Microsoft Signed Executable\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eUpon execution the Shellcode Loader at first disables the Event Tracing for Windows (ETW) by patching the WinApi “EtwEventWrite()” which is responsible for writing an event. ETW Patching process:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eRetrieves module handle of ntdll.dll via GetModuleHandleA\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eRetrieves address of EtwEventWrite via GetProcAddress\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_16.png\" alt=\"Fig 16. Fetches the address of EtwEventWrite\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 16. Fetches the address of EtwEventWrite\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eFurther it changes the protection of the region via VirtualProtect and then overwrites the first 4 bytes of the EtwEventWrite with following bytes: 0x48,0x33,0xc0,0xc3 (xor rax,rax | ret)\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/fig_17.png\" alt=\"Fig 17. Overwriting bytes to patch EtwEventWrite\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 17. Overwriting bytes to patch EtwEventWrite\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBy patching the EtwEventWrite function the ETW will not be able to write any events thus disabling the ETW.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThen the payload AES decrypts the shellcode using CryptDecrypt() as shown in the screenshot below - in this case the Algorithm ID used is “0x00006610” - AES256\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_18.png\" alt=\"Fig 18. AES Decrypts the Shellcode via CryptDecrypt\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 18. AES Decrypts the Shellcode via CryptDecrypt\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOnce the Shellcode is decrypted, the Shellcode is executed via \u003cstrong\u003eCreateThreadpoolWait()\u003c/strong\u003e where at first it creates an event object in a signaled state via CreateEventA(), then allocates RWX memory via VirtualAlloc() and writes the Shellcode in the allocated memory. Further it creates a wait object using CreateThreadpoolWait, here the first argument - callback function is set to the address of the shellcode. Then it set’s the wait object via the NtApi “TpSetWait” and at last calls the WaitForSingleObject which once executed checks if the waitable object is in signaled state, as our event was created in signaled state the callback function is been executed i.e the decrypted shellcode is been executed and the control flow is been transferred to the shellcode.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_19.png\" alt=\"Fig 19. Shellcode execution via CreateThreadpoolWait\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 19. Shellcode execution via CreateThreadpoolWait\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eKaynLdr - Shellcode\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Shellcode in this case is the “KaynLdr” which is commissioned to reflectively load the Havoc’s Demon DLL implant by calling its entrypoint function. Once the Shellcode is executed it retrieves the image base of the Demon DLL which is embedded in the shellcode itself by executing the following inline assembly function called KaynCaller.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_20.png\" alt=\"Fig 20. Retrieves the Image Base of the Embedded Demon DLL\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 20. Retrieves the Image Base of the Embedded Demon DLL\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFurther the KaynLdr performs the API Hashing routine in order to resolve the virtual addresses of various NTAPI’s by walking the export address table of the ntdll.dll (Function: LdrFunctionAddr) and initially the virtual address of the NTDLL.dll is been retrieved by walking the Process Environment Block (Function: LdrFunctionAddr) as shown in the screenshot below\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_21.png\" alt=\"Fig 21. API Hashing Routine used by Havoc Demon\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 21. API Hashing Routine used by Havoc Demon\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eHere the hashing algorithm used is a modified version of “DJB2” algorithm based on the constant “5381” or “0x1505” as shown in the screenshot below.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_22.png\" alt=\"Fig 22. Modified DJB2 Hashing Algorithm used in the API Hashing Routine\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 22. Modified DJB2 Hashing Algorithm used in the API Hashing Routine\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eVirtual Addresses for the following module and NTAPI’s are retrieved by using the API Hashing routine where the hardcoded DJB2 hashes are compared with the dynamically generated hash.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003cdiv\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\u003ctbody\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003e0x70e61753\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003entdll.dll\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003e0x9e456a43\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eLdrLoadDll\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003e0xf783b8ec\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eNtAllocateVirtualMemory\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003e0x50e92888\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eNtProtectVirtualMemory\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003c/tbody\u003e\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFurther the Embedded Demon DLL is memory mapped and the base relocations are calculated if required in an allocated memory page procured by calling the NtAllocateVirtualMemory(). Also the page protections are changed via multiple calls to NtProtectVirtualMemory as shown below.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_23.png\" alt=\"Fig 23. Memory Mapping of the embedded Demon DLL\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 23. Memory Mapping of the embedded Demon DLL\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Demon DLL is memory mapped in the Allocated memory without the DOS and NT Headers in order to evade detection mechanisms.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_24.png\" alt=\"Fig 24. Demon DLL is memory mapped without DOS and NT Headers\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 24. Demon DLL is memory mapped without DOS and NT Headers\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNow once the Demon DLL is memory mapped the KaynDllMain i.e the entrypoint of the DLL is executed by the KaynLdr as shown below, from there on the control is transferred to the Havoc Demon DLL Implant.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_25.png\" alt=\"Fig 25. Entrypoint of the Demon DLL is been executed by the KaynLdr\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 25. Entrypoint of the Demon DLL is been executed by the KaynLdr\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAnalysis of Havoc Demon DLL\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe entrypoint of the Havoc Demon DLL is executed by the KaynLdr as discussed previously. Now as the Havoc Demon has many features, we will only focus on a few of them in the following blog, as the features can be deduced from its source at: \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/HavocFramework/Havoc\"\u003e\u003cu\u003ehttps://github.com/HavocFramework/Havoc\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSo once the Havoc Demon is been executed there are four functions which are been executed by the DemonMain(): \u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDemonInit\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDemonMetaData\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDemonConfig\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDemonRoutine\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe DemonInit is the initialization function which\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eRetrieves the virtual addresses of functions from modules such as ntdll.dll/kernel32.dll by calling the API Hashing Routine discussed previously.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eRetrevies Syscall stubs for various NTAPI’s\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eLoads various Modules via walking the PEB with stacked strings\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eInitialize Session and Config Objects such as Demon AgentID, ProcessArch etc.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eNow let’s understand how the Configuration is being parsed via the DemonConfig() function.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Demon’s Configuration is been stored in the .data section as shown in the screenshot below\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_26.png\" alt=\"Fig 26. Demon Configuration stored in the .data section\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 26. Demon Configuration stored in the .data section\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe DemonConfig function parses the configuration by indexing the various required values from the config. Following is the configuration for the Demon DLL used in the campaign.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConfiguration:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eSleep: 2 (0x2)\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eInjection: \u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eAllocate: Native/Syscall (0x2)\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eExecute: Native/Syscall (0x2)\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSpawn:\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003ex64: C:\\Windows\\System32\\notepad.exe\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ex86: C:\\Windows\\SysWOW64\\notepad.exe\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSleep Obfuscation Technique: Ekko (0x2)\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eMethod: POST\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eHost: 146[.]190[.]48[.]229\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eTransport Secure: TRUE\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eUserAgent: Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 6.1; WOW64) AppleWebKit/537/36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/96.0.4664.110 Safari/537.36\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_27.png\" alt=\"Fig 27. Demon Configuration - Host (CnC) and UserAgent parsed\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 27. Demon Configuration - Host (CnC) and UserAgent parsed\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe \u003cstrong\u003eDemonRoutine()\u003c/strong\u003e function is the main loop for the malware, it is responsible for connecting to the command and control (C2) server, waiting for tasks from the server, executing those tasks, and then waiting again for more tasks and running indefinitely. It does the following things:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eFirst, it checks if it is connected to the C2 server. If not, it calls TransportInit() to connect to the server.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIf the connection is successful, it enters the CommandDispatcher() function, which is responsible for a task routine which parses the tasks and executes them until there are no more tasks in the queue.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIf the malware is unable to connect to the C2 server, it will keep trying to connect to the server again\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eNow let’s understand how it connects to the TransportInit function:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTransportInit() is responsible for connecting to the C2 server and establishing a session. It first sends the AES encrypted MetaData packet i.e the Check-in request generated via the DemonMetaData() function through the PackageTransmit() function, which could be sending data over HTTP or SMB, depending on the value of the TRANSPORT_HTTP or TRANSPORT_SMB macro. If the transmission is successful, it then decrypts the received data using AES encryption with a given key and initialization vector on the TeamServer. The decrypted data is then checked against the agent's ID, and if they match, the session is marked as connected and the function returns true.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_28.png\" alt=\"Fig 28. Metadata Structure - CheckIn Request\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 28. Metadata Structure - CheckIn Request\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTransportSend() is used to send data to the C2 server. It takes a pointer to the data and its size as input, and optionally returns received data and its size.It then creates a buffer with the data to be sent, and depending on the transport method, it either sends the data over HTTP or SMB.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_29.jpeg\" alt=\"Fig 29. TransportSend Function Arguments With Encrypted Data of the Check In request\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 29. TransportSend Function Arguments With Encrypted Data of the Check In request\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn the Teamserver end the CheckIn request with the metadata packet is been decrypted and showcased on the terminal with both encrypted and decrypted details of packets sent and received.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_30.png\" alt=\"Fig 30. Check In Request - Metadata packet parsed by the Team Server\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 30. Check In Request - Metadata packet parsed by the Team Server\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCommand Execution:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAfter the demon is deployed successfully on the target’s machine, the server is able to execute various commands on the target system. If the command \"whoami\" is issued to the payload, it would trigger the execution of the command and display the current user running the session.The server logs the command and its response upon execution.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_31.png\" alt=\"Fig 31. Command execution using Havoc GUI\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 31. Command execution using Havoc GUI\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOnce the command is executed on the victim machine, the command output is AES Encrypted and then sent to the CnC server, which is then decrypted by the TeamServer as shown in the screenshot below.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_32.jpeg\" alt=\"Fig 32. Command Output Logs parsed by the TeamServer\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 32. Command Output Logs parsed by the TeamServer\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eList Of Commands:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe specific commands available in Havoc will depend on the version and configuration of the framework, but some common commands that are often included in C2 frameworks include:\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/pic_33.png\" alt=\"Fig 33. Commands List\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFig 33. Commands List\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFurther the Demon implements various techniques mentioned below which can be analyzed from the \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/HavocFramework/Havoc\"\u003e\u003cu\u003esource\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eReturn Address Stack Spoofing\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIn-Direct Syscalls\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSleep Masking Techniques\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eEkko\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFOLIAGE\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eWaitForSingleObjectEx\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\n"},"fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":null}]},{"__typename":"ParagraphBlogContainer","fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":"Tracking The Threat Actor","fieldReusableParagraphs":[{"__typename":"ParagraphWysiwygModule","fieldWysiwygModContent":{"__typename":"Text","value":"\u003ch2\u003eTracking The Threat Actor - Infrastructure and Opsec Blunders\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe domain name “ttwweatterarartgea[.]ga” from where the final havoc demon payload “image.exe” is downloaded in this case resolves to the IP Address “146[.]190[.]48[.]229” - which is the IP address from where the final payload “pics.exe” was downloaded via the URL: http[:]//146[.]190[.]48[.]229/pics.exe previously.
